    1. Internal carotid
    2. External carotid
    3. Occipital
    4. Maxillary

    Answer: B. The external carotid artery divides into two branches: the maxillary artery and the superficial temporal artery.

37.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • The sensory and motor areas of the brain are somatotopically organized, with the lower extremity medial, upper extremity lateral, and head and neck most lateral.
  • The common carotid bifurcates at the hyoid bone (C3–C4) into the external and internal carotid arteries.
  • The carotid sheath contains the common and internal carotid arteries, internal jugular vein, vagus nerve, and ansa cervicalis.
  • The middle cerebral artery is most commonly affected by emboli, causing deficits in movement, sensation, and language.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:927–32, 1067–72.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 105, 136, 142.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:750–1.

Chapter 38: Torticollis

38.1 Approach to the Triangle Neck: Anterior

Learning Objectives:

  • Identify surface landmarks of the anterior neck.
  • Describe the actions of the sternocleidomastoid muscle (SCM).

Definitions:

  • Fibrosis: Abnormal growth of fibrous connective tissue in response to trauma or infection.
  • Anterior neck: Portion of the neck anterior to the trapezius muscle, containing anterior and posterior triangles divided by the SCM.

The sternocleidomastoid muscle (SCM) is a key surface landmark, dividing the anterior neck into anterior and posterior triangles. Superiorly, it attaches to the mastoid process of the temporal bone; inferiorly, it splits to attach to the manubrium of the sternum and the clavicle. Contraction of the SCM rotates the head to the opposite side and causes lateral flexion. Co-contraction of both SCMs contributes to straight neck flexion. The SCM is innervated by the spinal accessory nerve (CN XI), which also innervates the trapezius.

Other landmarks include the laryngeal prominence (thyroid cartilage), external jugular vein (crossing the SCM to drain into the internal jugular), and platysma muscle folds. The external jugular vein forms from the posterior auricular and retromandibular veins, and the great auricular nerve crosses the SCM.

38.2 Clinical Case: Torticollis

Case Study:

A 3-month-old girl is noted by the pediatrician to have a stiff neck for a 2-month duration. The mother states that the neck seems to be pulled to the right. On examination, the baby’s right ear is tilted toward her right side, but the baby’s face is turned toward the left. Palpation of the neck reveals a nontender mass of the right anterior neck region.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Torticollis.
  • Anatomical structure affected: Sternocleidomastoid muscle (SCM).

Summary: A 3-month-old girl’s head is flexed to the right and rotated to the left. Palpation reveals a nontender mass in the right anterior neck region.

Clinical Correlation: Torticollis, observed in 3–5 per 1000 births, is due to fibrosis of the SCM, causing muscle shortening. A palpable mass (about olive-sized) is present in 66% of cases. It may be associated with breech births or difficult deliveries, leading to lateral head flexion and contralateral rotation. Physical therapy is effective in most cases, with surgery rarely needed.

38.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. A 2-year-old girl is diagnosed with torticollis involving the right SCM. Which of the following describes the most likely anatomical change?
    1. Head flexed forward in the midline
    2. Head rotated to the right
    3. Head rotated to the left
    4. Head extended in the midline plane

    Answer: C. With torticollis, the SCM is shortened, leading to the head being rotated toward the contralateral side.

  2. A 24-year-old football player receives a blow to the left skull, and the team physician finds weakness of the left SCM. Which of the following associated findings is most likely to be seen in this patient?
    1. Weakness of the masseter muscle
    2. Decreased sensation of the ipsilateral face
    3. Decreased tearing from the ipsilateral eye
    4. Weakness of the trapezius muscle

    Answer: D. The SCM and the trapezius muscle are innervated by the spinal accessory nerve (CN XI), which is at risk in the posterior triangle of the neck.

  3. A clinician is palpating the anterior neck of a patient who has been involved in a motor vehicle accident and notes the laryngeal prominence. Which of the following describes the anatomical structure that corresponds to this prominence?
    1. Thyroid cartilage
    2. Cricoid cartilage
    3. Hyoid bone
    4. First tracheal ring

    Answer: A. The laryngeal prominence is produced by the superior border of the thyroid cartilage.

38.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • The anterior neck contains structures that lie anterior to the trapezius muscle.
  • The SCM divides the anterior neck into anterior and posterior triangles.
  • Contraction of the SCM causes rotation of the head to the opposite side and lateral flexion.
  • The laryngeal prominence in the midline is formed by the superior border of the thyroid cartilage.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:1052–8.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 28, 127.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:735–42.

Chapter 39: Metastatic Scalene Node

39.1 Approach to the Neck: Lymphatics

Learning Objectives:

  • Describe general patterns of lymphatic drainage in the body.
  • Distinguish lymphatic flow through the supraclavicular nodes from flow through other nodes in the neck.

Definitions:

  • Hemoptysis: Coughing up blood.
  • Palpation: Technique of physical examination that uses the hands.
  • Metastasis: Spread of disease from one part of the body to another, usually used to describe the spread of cancer cells.

The lymphatic system complements the venous system, returning serum constituents to the heart. Lymphatic vessels form from tissue plexuses, interrupted by lymph nodes containing white blood cells. Lymphatics from below the diaphragm drain into the cisterna chyli and then the thoracic duct, which ascends in the posterior mediastinum to drain into the venous system at the junction of the left subclavian and internal jugular veins. Above the diaphragm, left-side lymphatics drain into the thoracic duct, while right-side lymphatics drain into the right lymphatic duct, which empties into the right subclavian vein.

In the neck, lymphatic flow is from superficial to deep, paralleling major veins. Superficial and deep lymph node clusters drain from superior to inferior. The inferior deep group, along the internal jugular and subclavian veins, also drains the upper extremity, thorax, and abdomen. Supraclavicular nodes are sentinel nodes, often enlarging first in cancers from the thorax or abdomen due to lymphatic spread.

39.2 Clinical Case: Metastatic Scalene Node

Case Study:

A 67-year-old man is noted to be coughing up bright red blood for a period of 1 week. He denies exposure to tuberculosis but has smoked one pack of cigarettes per day for 30 years. On examination, his lungs are clear. Palpation of the supraclavicular regions shows a hard nontender irregular mass on the left side.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Lung cancer with a left-side supraclavicular metastatic node.
  • Anatomical explanation for this particular mass: Lymphatic drainage through the thoracic duct to the left brachiocephalic vein.

Summary: A 67-year-old man who has smoked cigarettes for 30 years has a 1-week history of hemoptysis. Palpation of the supraclavicular regions shows a hard nontender irregular mass on the left side.

Clinical Correlation: This smoker’s hemoptysis is suspicious for lung cancer. The hard irregular mass in the left supraclavicular region is likely malignant metastasis to lymph nodes, common on the left due to lymphatic drainage through the thoracic duct into the left subclavian vein.

39.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. A 57-year-old man is diagnosed with colon cancer. He is noted to have a probable metastatic mass in the neck at the thoracic duct. Where is the metastasis likely to be located?
    1. Right supraclavicular region
    2. Right subclavicular region
    3. Left supraclavicular region
    4. Left subclavicular area

    Answer: C. The thoracic duct drains into the left subclavian vein. Malignant metastasis is often diverted to the supraclavicular nodes, where it proliferates.

  2. A 65-year-old woman is noted to have cancer of the vulva. It is noted that the cancer has spread to the lymph nodes in the femoral triangle. Which of the following best describes the location of the lymph nodes?
    1. Immediately lateral to the femoral nerve
    2. Immediately medial to the femoral nerve
    3. Immediately medial to the femoral artery
    4. Immediately medial to the femoral vein
    5. Immediately lateral to the femoral vein

    Answer: D. The relations of the structures in the groin can be remembered by the mnemonic NAVEL: Nerve, Artery, Vein, Empty space, Lymph node.

  3. What is the mechanism that propels lymph through the lymphatic vessels?
    1. Cardiac contractility
    2. Gravity
    3. Peristalsis
    4. Compression

    Answer: D. Compression is the primary way that lymph moves through the lymphatic vessels.

39.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • Lymphatic vessels are not continuous channels but are interrupted by lymph nodes, which contain dense aggregations of white blood cells (lymphocytes).
  • In general, lymphatics from below the diaphragm on both sides of the body drain into the cisterna chyli and then to the thoracic duct.
  • The lymph ascends in the posterior mediastinum to drain into the venous system at the junction between the left brachiocephalic and internal jugular veins.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:181, 1108–14.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plate 72.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:754–7.

Chapter 40: Bell Palsy

40.1 Approach to the Facial Nerve

Learning Objectives:

  • Describe the course of the facial nerve (CN VII).
  • List the functional components of the facial nerve.

Definitions:

  • Bell palsy: Idiopathic palsy of peripheral CN VII leading to ipsilateral facial weakness.
  • Chorda tympani: Small branch of the facial nerve that supplies taste receptors in the anterior two-thirds of the tongue.
  • Upper motor neuron: Neurons that conduct information from motor areas of the brain to the spinal cord. The lower motor neurons project from gray matter in the spinal cord to peripheral muscle.
  • Vidian nerve: Nerve of the pterygoid canal.
  • Branchiomeric muscle: Skeletal muscle derived from one of the branchial arches, generally innervated by cranial nerves.

The facial nerve (CN VII) originates from the lateral surface of the caudal pons at the cerebellopontine junction, with a large branchiomeric motor root and a smaller nervus intermedius containing sensory and visceral motor fibers. It enters the internal acoustic meatus with the vestibulocochlear nerve (CN VIII), occupying the anterosuperior quadrant. The nerve bends posteriorly at the genu within the facial canal, where the greater petrosal nerve and geniculate ganglion arise. It passes through the facial canal, inferior to the lateral semicircular canal, and gives off branches to the stapedius muscle and chorda tympani before exiting via the stylomastoid foramen.

The facial nerve courses through the parotid gland, splitting into temporal, zygomatic, buccal, mandibular, and cervical branches, plus a posterior auricular branch. The greater petrosal nerve courses anteriorly to the pterygopalatine fossa, merging with the deep petrosal nerve to form the Vidian nerve, which synapses in the pterygopalatine ganglion to innervate glands. The chorda tympani supplies taste to the anterior two-thirds of the tongue and parasympathetic fibers to the submandibular and sublingual glands.

The facial nerve’s functions include motor innervation to facial expression muscles, stapedius, stylohyoid, and posterior digastric; visceral motor innervation to lacrimal, submandibular, sublingual, and mucosal glands; and sensory innervation for taste (anterior two-thirds of the tongue) and a small patch of auricular skin.

40.2 Clinical Case: Bell Palsy

Case Study:

A 28-year-old woman at 19 weeks of pregnancy complains of acute onset of numbness of the right cheek and drooping of the right face that occurred over 1 hour. She denies trauma to the head. On examination, the patient has difficulty closing her right eyelid, and her right nasolabial fold is smoother than on the left. She also is drooling from the right side of her mouth. The remainder of the neurological examination is normal.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Bell palsy (idiopathic seventh cranial nerve palsy).
  • Anatomical mechanism for this condition: Dysfunction of the peripheral portion of the seventh cranial nerve.

Summary: A 28-year-old woman at 19 weeks of pregnancy has acute onset of right cheek numbness and facial drooping, with difficulty closing her right eyelid and blunting of the right nasolabial fold.

Clinical Correlation: Bell palsy is an idiopathic facial nerve paralysis causing unilateral facial weakness, affecting taste, orbicularis oculi, and orbicularis oris muscles. It involves both upper and lower face, distinguishing it from upper motor neuron lesions. Pregnancy increases incidence. Treatment includes eye protection and corticosteroids, with full recovery typical.

40.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. A 44-year-old man complains of difficulty hearing from the right ear and headaches. He also has weakness of the facial muscles. Which of the following is the most likely explanation?
    1. Peripheral CN VII palsy
    2. Peripheral CN VIII palsy
    3. Cerebellar pontine angle lesion
    4. Trigeminal ganglion lesion

    Answer: C. When multiple nerves are affected, it is unlikely to be a peripheral disorder. Cranial nerves VII and VIII exit in close proximity from the pons. A schwannoma involving the cerebellopontine angle can affect both cranial nerves.

  2. An injury to the facial nerve (CN VII) as it leaves the stylomastoid foramen would disrupt which function?
    1. Taste to the posterior tongue
    2. Sensation to the cornea
    3. Sensation to the cheek
    4. Sensation to the anterior scalp
    5. Wrinkling the forehead

    Answer: E. Wrinkling the forehead is produced by contraction of the frontalis muscle, which is innervated by the facial nerve. The facial nerve is responsible for taste in the anterior two-thirds of the tongue, but the chorda tympani emerges before the main trunk exits through the stylomastoid foramen. Sensation of the cornea and sensation to the cheek are supplied by the trigeminal nerve.

  3. A 33-year-old woman suffered a skull fracture that led to a unilateral facial nerve palsy. Which of the following fractures was most likely responsible?
    1. Frontal calvaria
    2. Temporal bone fracture involving the squamous part
    3. Occipital fracture
    4. Basilar fracture involving the mastoid area

    Answer: D. The basilar fracture involving the mastoid region of the temporal bone may impinge on the facial nerve as it exits the stylomastoid foramen.

40.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • Sensory fibers in the chorda tympani course with branches of the lingual nerve to supply taste receptors in the anterior two-thirds of the tongue.
  • The facial nerve supplies most of the muscles involved with facial expression but also supplies the stapedius, stylohyoid, and posterior belly of the digastric muscle.
  • CN VII carries visceral motor neurons that supply the lacrimal gland, the submandibular and sublingual salivary glands, and mucus-secreting glands of the nasal and oral cavities.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:933–47, 1143–46.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 25, 123.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:732–4, 763–5.

Chapter 41: Trigeminal Neuralgia

41.1 Approach to Trigeminal Nerve

Learning Objectives:

  • Relate the dermatomes of the face to the branches of the trigeminal nerve (CN V).
  • List the functions of the trigeminal nerve.

Definitions:

  • Multiple sclerosis: Disease in which plaques in the nervous system arise because of the proliferation of fibrous connective tissue or glial cells.
  • Baclofen: Muscle relaxing drug that acts through type b γ-amino butyric acid (GABAb) receptors.
  • Carbamazepine: Centrally acting anticonvulsive drug of unknown action.

The trigeminal nerve (CN V) exits the brain from the lateral surface of the pons. The sensory fibers arise as a large root, while motor fibers to the muscles of mastication usually arise as a separate smaller root. The nerve courses on the lateral surface of the sphenoid bone deep to the cavernous sinus. The cell bodies of the sensory nerves form the trigeminal ganglion along the medial wall of the middle cranial fossa. Three large nerves emerge from the ganglion: the ophthalmic (V1), maxillary (V2), and mandibular (V3) divisions.

Branches of these nerves supply general sensation to the face and anterior scalp, while the posterior scalp is supplied by cervical spinal nerves. The ophthalmic nerve supplies the dermatome superior to the horizontal midline of the orbit and the midline region of the nose. The maxillary nerve supplies the region over the maxilla, inferior to the orbit, including the lateral surface of the nose and the upper lip, with a small band extending superiorly over the zygomatic arch and temporalis muscle. The mandibular division supplies a band of skin running superiorly over the temporalis muscle.

The major branches of the ophthalmic nerve supplying the skin are the supraorbital and supratrochlear nerves (forehead and anterior scalp) and the nasociliary nerve (medial nose via the external nasal branch of the anterior ethmoidal nerve). The maxillary nerve innervates the skin primarily through the infraorbital nerve, with contributions from the zygomaticofacial and zygomaticotemporal nerves. The mandibular nerve’s branches include the auriculotemporal nerve (superiorly), mental nerve (inferiorly, a branch of the inferior alveolar), and buccal nerve (cheek skin and buccal mucosa, but not motor innervation to the buccinator, which is supplied by CN VII).

41.2 Clinical Case: Trigeminal Neuralgia

Case Study:

A 35-year-old woman complains of spasms of excruciating pain of the right cheek and chin. These pain episodes last for a few seconds and are intense. She had been diagnosed with multiple sclerosis 2 years previously. She is not taking medications currently, although she previously received intravenous corticosteroid therapy. Her physician says that her problem is related to the nerve that innervates the skin of the cheek area.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Trigeminal neuralgia.
  • Anatomical explanation for this condition: Pain follows the distribution of CN V, which innervates the eyes, cheeks, and chin.

Summary: A 35-year-old woman who has multiple sclerosis complains of excruciating spasms of pain that affect her right cheek and chin and last for a few seconds.

Clinical Correlation: Trigeminal neuralgia (tic douloureux) is among the most excruciating types of pain, causing patients to wince. This woman’s history of multiple sclerosis is significant, as trigeminal neuralgia is relatively common in this group. The pain’s character excludes migraine (throbbing unilateral pain with orbital involvement) or tension headache (band-like constricting pain). She has no history of herpes simplex virus, which can affect CN V. CN V has three sensory branches. Treatment includes carbamazepine or baclofen and, in severe cases, trigeminal nerve ablation.

41.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. A 56-year-old man had a stroke. Among other symptoms, a marked deficit in bite strength was observed on the affected side, indicating weakness in the muscles of mastication. Which of the following muscles is also innervated by the same nerve?
    1. Orbicularis oculi
    2. Platysma
    3. Anterior belly of digastric
    4. Stylohyoid
    5. Superior belly of omohyoid

    Answer: C. The anterior belly of digastric is innervated by CN V3, as are the muscles of mastication. The platysma and orbicularis oculi are supplied by CN VII.

  2. A 45-year-old woman who has diabetes has developed shingles involving the right cornea. Through which nerve did the varicella virus most likely travel to the cornea?
    1. CN II
    2. CN III
    3. CN V
    4. CN VII

    Answer: C. The trigeminal nerve supplies sensory innervation to the cornea. Herpes simplex or varicella virus infections involving the face may travel through CN V to the cornea, endangering vision.

  3. Match the following divisions (A–C) to the branches [41.3–41.6].
    1. CN V1
    2. CN V2
    3. CN V3

    [41.3] Auriculotemporal nerve

    [41.4] Lacrimal nerve

    [41.5] Supraorbital nerve

    [41.6] Infraorbital nerve

    Answers:

    [41.3] C. CN V3 supplies the auriculotemporal, buccal, and mental nerves.

    [41.4] A. CN V1 supplies the lacrimal, supraorbital, and supra- and infratrochlear nerves.

    [41.5] A. CN V1 supplies the lacrimal, supraorbital, and supra- and infratrochlear nerves.

    [41.6] B. CN V2 supplies the infraorbital and zygomaticotemporal nerves.

41.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • The trigeminal nerve exits the brain from the lateral surface of the pons.
  • The trigeminal nerve is comprised of three divisions: ophthalmic, maxillary, and mandibular.
  • Branches of these nerves supply general sensation to the face and anterior scalp. The posterior scalp is supplied by cervical spinal nerves.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:939–45, 1139–42.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 24, 122.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:727–9, 759–62.

Chapter 42: Oculomotor Nerve Palsy

42.1 Approach to Extraocular Muscles of the Orbit

Learning Objectives:

  • Name the seven extraocular eye muscles of each orbit, their attachments, actions, and innervation.
  • Describe how each of these muscles is optimally tested in a clinical setting.
  • Describe the presentation of a patient with injury to each of the nerves innervating these muscles.

Definitions:

  • Ptosis: Drooping or partial closure of the upper eyelid.
  • Nerve palsy: Partial or incomplete paralysis.
  • Aneurysm: Dilatation of the wall of an artery due to an acquired or congenital condition.

The extraocular muscles of the orbit include the levator palpebrae superioris, four rectus muscles (superior, inferior, lateral, and medial), and two oblique muscles (superior and inferior). All originate from the apex of the pyramidal orbit near the optic canal, except the inferior oblique, which arises from the anterior orbital floor. All but the levator palpebrae superioris attach to the eyeball, controlling its movement. Their attachments, actions, and innervation are detailed in Table 42-1.

Table 42-1: Extraocular Muscles of the Orbit
MuscleOriginInsertionActionInnervation
Superior obliquePosterior orbitPosterosuperior scleraDepresses and abductsCN IV
Inferior obliqueAnterior orbital floorPosteroinferior scleraElevates and abductsCN III
Superior rectusCommon tendinous ringAnterior scleraElevates and adductsCN III
Inferior rectusCommon tendinous ringAnterior scleraDepresses and adductsCN III
Lateral rectusCommon tendinous ringAnterior scleraAbductsCN VI
Medial rectusCommon tendinous ringAnterior scleraAdductsCN III

The levator palpebrae superioris elevates the upper eyelid, attaching to the skin and tarsal plate, opposed by the orbicularis oculi. It contains smooth muscle fibers (superior tarsal muscle) innervated by sympathetic nerves. The superior oblique passes through the trochlea, a pulley at the superior-medial orbit, inserting on the posterosuperior sclera, turning the pupil down and out. The inferior oblique inserts on the posteroinferior sclera, turning the pupil up and out. The rectus muscles originate from a common tendinous ring, inserting on the anterior sclera, with superior and inferior recti also adducting and rotating the eyeball medially.

For testing, the superior and inferior recti are assessed with the eyeball abducted, aligning their pull for elevation and depression. The superior and inferior obliques are tested with the eyeball adducted, aligning their pull for depression and elevation. The lateral and medial recti are tested by abducting or adducting the eyeball. Oculomotor nerve (CN III) injury results in a pupil turned down and out, dilated, with ptosis. Trochlear nerve (CN IV) injury causes slight adduction, weak downward gaze, and head tilting. Abducens nerve (CN VI) injury results in an adducted eye.

42.2 Clinical Case: Oculomotor Nerve Palsy

Case Study:

A 38-year-old male presents to the emergency room with complaints of a persistent headache and problems with his left eye. He has no known medical problems, and his headache is slightly improved with ibuprofen. He denies having any previous problems with his vision. On exam, he is noted to have a left eye with ptosis, dilated pupil, and displacement “down and out.” The remainder of the exam is normal. An MRI shows an aneurysm of the circle of Willis.

  • Ocular muscles not involved: Superior oblique and lateral rectus.
  • Nerve affected: Oculomotor nerve (CN III).

Summary: A 38-year-old healthy male with new onset left eye findings of ptosis, dilated pupil, and displacement of eye “down and out.”

Clinical Correlation: Ptosis, dilated pupil, and eye displacement “down and out” indicate oculomotor nerve palsy. The oculomotor nerve (CN III), originating from the midbrain, controls most eye movements, pupil constriction, and eyelid elevation. The unopposed action of the lateral rectus (CN VI) and superior oblique (CN IV) causes the “down and out” position. Causes include aneurysm, compression, infection, infarction, or tumor.

42.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. During a physical examination of a patient, you notice ptosis of the patient’s left eye. This would indicate to you there is paralysis of which muscle?
    1. Orbicularis muscle
    2. Superior oblique muscle
    3. Inferior oblique muscle
    4. Inferior rectus muscle
    5. Levator palpebrae muscle

    Answer: E. Ptosis or drooping of the eyelid is due to paralysis of the levator palpebrae muscle. The orbicularis muscle closes the eyelid.

  2. While performing a physical examination, you test the function of the muscles attached to the eyeball, and thereby the integrity of their innervation. You would test the function of the superior oblique muscle by having the patient do which of the following?
    1. Look in toward the nose
    2. Look laterally
    3. Look in toward the nose and then upward
    4. Look in toward the nose and then down
    5. Look laterally and then down

    Answer: D. Turning the eyeball inward places the superior oblique’s axis in line with its pull, depressing the pupil.

  3. During this same physical exam, you ask the patient to look laterally with her right eye, and then upward. You have just tested the function of which muscle?
    1. Superior rectus muscle
    2. Superior oblique muscle
    3. Inferior oblique muscle
    4. Inferior rectus muscle
    5. Medial rectus muscle

    Answer: A. Turning the eyeball out aligns the superior rectus muscle’s pull, elevating the eyeball.

42.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • LR6 SO4 AO3: Lateral rectus, CN VI; superior oblique, CN IV; all others, CN III.
  • The functional portion of the superior oblique muscle is between the trochlea and the insertion of the tendon.
  • The oculomotor nerve (CN III) innervates the majority of the extraocular muscles, the sphincter muscle of the pupil, and the smooth muscle fibers of the superior tarsal muscle.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:968–72.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders, 2006: plates 84, 86.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:691, 699–701.

Chapter 43: Cephalohematoma

43.1 Approach to the Scalp and Skull

Learning Objectives:

  • Define the layers of the scalp.
  • Describe the structure of cranial sutures.

Definitions:

  • Major sutures of the skull: The sagittal suture runs along the midline between the parietal bones. The lambdoidal suture separates the parietal bones from the occipital bone posteriorly. The coronal suture separates the frontal bone from the parietal bones anteriorly.
  • Hematoma: Pool of blood that accumulates in a tissue or space, usually clotted.
  • Bilirubin: Bile salt formed from hemoglobin breakdown by the liver, causing jaundice at high blood levels.
  • Icteric: Yellowish appearance of jaundice, here due to bilirubin breakdown in the hematoma.
  • Caput succedaneum: Edematous swelling of the superficial scalp due to birth trauma, resolving within 2–3 days.

The scalp covers the calvaria, consisting of five layers remembered by the mnemonic SCALP: Skin, Connective tissue (dense), Aponeurosis (galea aponeurotica), Loose connective tissue, and Periosteum. The skin, dense connective tissue, and aponeurosis move together, while the loose connective tissue allows superficial layers to slide over the periosteum, which adheres tightly to the bone, especially at sutures in infants.

The scalp’s blood supply comes from the internal carotid (supraorbital and supratrochlear arteries) and external carotid (superficial temporal, occipital, and posterior auricular arteries), with extensive anastomoses. Sensory innervation anteriorly is via CN V (supraorbital, supratrochlear, auriculotemporal nerves), and posteriorly by cervical spinal nerves (C2: greater occipital; C3; lesser occipital, posterior auricular from cervical plexus).

Trauma can cause hematomas. Superficial fascia hematomas spread slowly due to septa, while subperiosteal hematomas in infants are contained by sutures, as seen in cephalohematomas from suction-assisted delivery. In adults, the loose connective layer is a “danger space” for infection spread to the periorbital space and cranium via the cavernous sinus.

43.2 Clinical Case: Cephalohematoma

Case Study:

A male infant who weighs 3500 g appears icteric on examination. The previous day, the infant was delivered vaginally by vacuum-assisted extraction because there were severe fetal heart rate decelerations. The infant’s scalp has a 5-cm discolored soft tissue swelling that seems to be contained by and does not cross the sagittal or lambdoidal sutures. The mother had no prenatal or medical problems. There is no family history of bleeding disorders.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Cephalohematoma.
  • Anatomical mechanism for the condition: Injury to the branches of arteries supplying the lateral skull.

Summary: A 1-day-old male infant, delivered by vacuum-assisted extraction, appears icteric with a 5-cm scalp hematoma contained within the sagittal and lambdoidal sutures.

Clinical Correlation: The infant’s icterus and suture-contained soft tissue mass indicate a cephalohematoma, distinct from caput succedaneum, which crosses sutures. Hemoglobin in the hematoma breaks down into bilirubin, causing icterus. Vacuum extraction likely injured periosteal arteries, leading to subperiosteal bleeding. A skull radiograph or CT scan assesses for fractures. Most cephalohematomas resolve with observation.

43.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. Which of the following best describes the layers of scalp?
    1. Skin, aponeurosis, dense connective tissue, periosteum
    2. Skin, loose connective tissue, aponeurosis, periosteum
    3. Skin, dense connective tissue, aponeurosis, loose connective tissue, periosteum
    4. Skin, aponeurosis, loose connective tissue, muscle, periosteum

    Answer: C. The layers of the scalp are Skin, Connective tissue, Aponeurosis, Loose connective tissue, Periosteum (SCALP).

  2. A 65-year-old woman complains of severe pain of the right side of the head. A vascular surgeon takes a biopsy of the artery deep to the temporalis muscle. Which of the following vessels did the surgeon most likely biopsy?
    1. Middle meningeal artery
    2. External carotid artery
    3. Ophthalmic artery
    4. Deep temporal artery

    Answer: D. The deep temporal artery lies deep to the temporalis muscle and is associated with temporal arteritis, causing headache and joint pain.

  3. A neurologist uses a pin to test the sensation to a 26-year-old man’s scalp just near the hairline anteriorly. Which of the following nerves provides the innervation to the scalp in this region?
    1. CN V
    2. CN VII
    3. CN X
    4. Spinal nerve C2

    Answer: A. The anterior scalp is innervated by CN V, while the posterior scalp is innervated by spinal nerve C2.

43.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • The layers of the scalp are remembered by SCALP: Skin, Connective tissue, Aponeurosis, Loose connective tissue, Periosteum.
  • The scalp’s blood supply comes from branches of the internal and external carotid arteries.
  • Sensory innervation of the scalp is by CN V anteriorly (supraorbital, supratrochlear, auriculotemporal nerves) and spinal nerves C2 and C3 posteriorly. C1 has no sensory component.
  • Infants undergoing suction-assisted delivery may develop subperiosteal hematomas due to periosteal artery damage.

References:

  • Bickley LS, Szilagyi PG. Bate’s Guide to the Physical Examination and History Taking, 8th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2003:667–8.
  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:906–8.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 12, 23.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:722–6.

Chapter 44: Epidural Hematoma

44.1 Approach to Meninges and Arterial Supply to Brain

Learning Objectives:

  • List the meningeal layers.
  • Identify the dural folds and associated dural sinuses.
  • Describe the vascular supplies to the meninges and underlying brain.

Definitions:

  • Pachymeninx: The thick meningeal layer, i.e., the dura mater.
  • Leptomeninx: The thin meningeal layers, i.e., the arachnoid and pia maters together.
  • Dural sinus: Cavity filled with venous blood formed by a split in the dura mater’s periosteal and meningeal layers, draining into the internal jugular vein.
  • Pterion: A landmark where the frontal, parietal, temporal, and sphenoid bones converge, often H-shaped.

The brain is covered by three meningeal layers: dura mater (pachymeninx), arachnoid mater, and pia mater (leptomeninges). The dura mater, a thick membrane, adheres to the cranium’s deep surface. The arachnoid mater, a thin layer, adheres to the dura, separated from the brain by the subarachnoid space containing cerebrospinal fluid (CSF). The pia mater adheres to the brain’s surface.

The dura mater has two layers: an external periosteal layer attached to bone and an internal meningeal layer forming folds. The falx cerebri separates cerebral hemispheres, the tentorium cerebelli separates cerebrum from cerebellum, and the falx cerebelli partially separates cerebellar lobes. The diaphragma sellae covers the pituitary fossa. Dural sinuses form where the layers split, including the superior sagittal sinus (along falx cerebri), transverse sinus (along tentorium cerebelli), and others, draining into the internal jugular vein.

The dura is supplied by the middle meningeal artery, arising from the maxillary artery, entering via the foramen spinosum, and running within the dura near the pterion. The brain is supplied by the circle of Willis, formed by internal carotid and vertebral arteries. Trauma can cause hematomas: epidural (middle meningeal artery rupture, between dura and cranium), subarachnoid (cerebral artery rupture), or subdural (venous rupture, between dura and arachnoid).

44.2 Clinical Case: Epidural Hematoma

Case Study:

A 15-year-old boy was the pitcher for his little league baseball team when he was hit by a line drive to the right temple area. He lost consciousness briefly but woke up after about 45 seconds and had no neurological deficits. He was taken to the emergency room and seemed to be in good condition. Four hours later, while being observed, he complained of an increasing headache and had a seizure. On examination, the patient’s right pupil appeared dilated and reacted sluggishly to light. The emergency physician is concerned about increased intracranial pressure.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Epidural hematoma resulting in increased intracranial pressure.
  • Anatomical explanation for this condition: Disruption of a branch of the middle meningeal artery, causing a growing hematoma between the dura and cranium, putting pressure on the brain.

Summary: A 15-year-old boy was hit by a baseball to the right temple, briefly lost consciousness, had a lucid interval, and later developed headache, seizure, and a dilated, sluggish right pupil, indicating increased intracranial pressure.

Clinical Correlation: The boy’s trauma likely disrupted the middle meningeal artery under the temporal bone, causing an epidural hematoma. The lucid interval followed by deterioration is typical, as arterial bleeding rapidly expands the hematoma, compressing the brain and CN III, affecting the ipsilateral pupil. Emergent decompression and surgical control of bleeding are critical.

44.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. A 35-year-old man developed an intracranial hemorrhage when one of the meningeal arteries ruptured. Anatomically, where is the hematoma located?
    1. Immediately superficial to the dura
    2. Immediately deep to the dura
    3. Within the subarachnoid space
    4. Within the brain parenchyma

    Answer: A. Injuries to the meningeal arteries lead to epidural hematomas.

  2. A 1-month-old infant is seen in the emergency department due to lethargy and seizures. After careful questioning, it was discovered that the infant was shaken before the change in mental status. Which vessels are most likely to be injured?
    1. Meningeal arteries
    2. Meningeal veins
    3. Emissary veins
    4. Middle cerebral arteries

    Answer: C. Shaken infants are vulnerable to emissary vein lacerations, leading to subdural hematomas.

  3. A 21-year-old man is brought into the emergency room after being hit in the head with a baseball bat. The neurosurgeon notes that the skull fracture and underlying hematoma seem to have occurred at the junction of the four major bones of the skull. Which of the following describes this region?
    1. Bregma
    2. Lambda
    3. Pterion
    4. Nasion

    Answer: C. The pterion is where the frontal, parietal, temporal, and sphenoid bones converge, and it is the thinnest part of the skull.

44.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • The dura mater consists of two layers: an external periosteal layer attached to bone and an internal meningeal layer.
  • The pterion is an external landmark for the middle meningeal artery.
  • Middle meningeal artery injuries lead to epidural hematomas.
  • Ruptured cerebral arteries cause subarachnoid hematomas.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:908–21.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 100–2.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:681–7.

Chapter 45: Salivary Stone

45.1 Approach to Salivary Glands

Learning Objectives:

  • Describe the salivary glands and the course of their ducts to the oral cavity.
  • Identify structures in the floor of the mouth related to the submandibular gland.

Definitions:

  • Caruncle: Small protuberance or papilla.
  • Frenulum: Mucosal fold extending along the midline from the floor of the mouth to the inferior surface of the tongue.

Three salivary glands form a ring around the oral cavity: parotid, submandibular, and sublingual. The parotid gland lies superficial and posterior to the mandible’s ramus, inferior to the ear. The submandibular gland lies below the mandible’s angle and body, superficial to the mylohyoid muscle. The sublingual gland lies in the floor of the mouth between the mandible and genioglossus muscle.

The parotid duct crosses the masseter muscle, pierces the buccinator, and opens into the oral cavity at the second upper molar. The submandibular duct arises from the gland’s deep lobe, runs anteriorly on the hyoglossus muscle, and opens via submandibular caruncles lateral to the lingual frenulum. The sublingual gland has multiple small ducts emptying at the tongue’s base.

The submandibular duct is related to the mylohyoid, hyoglossus, and sublingual gland. The hypoglossal nerve (CN XII) courses inferior to the duct, and the lingual nerve loops underneath it before innervating the tongue.

45.2 Clinical Case: Salivary Stone

Case Study:

A 36-year-old woman complains of pain and swelling beneath the left mandible, particularly after eating a meal. On examination, she is noted to have edema and tenderness of the left submandibular region. Palpation of her mouth reveals a 4-mm, irregular, nonmobile, hard mass in the mucosa of her mouth. She denies trauma to the region and does not have an eating disorder.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Stone in the submandibular duct (sialolithiasis).
  • Anatomical course of the affected structure: The submandibular salivary duct drains from the deep lobe of the submandibular gland and courses anterolaterally along the base of the tongue. Occlusion by a stone causes saliva to accumulate proximally, causing distention and pain.

Summary: A 36-year-old woman has pain and swelling of the left submandibular area, with tenderness and a 4-mm irregular mass along the floor of her mouth. She denies trauma or eating disorders.

Clinical Correlation: The woman’s post-meal pain and submandibular swelling suggest a stone in the submandibular duct. Saliva accumulation proximal to the occlusion causes duct or gland capsule stretching. Swelling may indicate secondary infection. Sialolithiasis results from a particle (e.g., food, bacteria) serving as a nucleus for material deposition. Sialoendoscopy is diagnostic, with treatment involving stone excision and antibiotics, or gland removal if necessary.

45.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. A 22-year-old male is involved in a knife fight after a soccer game. He is brought to the emergency department. An 8-cm laceration that involves the right cheek, from his right ear to near the corner of his mouth, is noted. Which of the following structures is most likely injured?
    1. Parotid duct
    2. Submandibular duct
    3. Superficial temporal artery
    4. Lingual artery
    5. Mandibular branch of the facial nerve

    Answer: A. The parotid duct and buccal branch of CN VII travel in the cheek, along a line from the tragus to the mouth’s corner.

  2. A 45-year-old woman is undergoing surgical resection of the salivary gland for probable cancer. After surgery, she notes that she cannot move her tongue well. Which of the following salivary glands is most likely involved in the surgery?
    1. Parotid
    2. Sublingual
    3. Submandibular
    4. Maxillary

    Answer: C. The hypoglossal nerve (CN XII) courses deep to the submandibular gland, and its injury weakens tongue muscles.

  3. A 16-year-old girl is brought into the physician’s office because her mother suspects an eating disorder. The patient has bilateral swelling of the cheeks that are nontender. There are multiple dental caries. She appears to be of normal weight. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?
    1. Anorexia nervosa
    2. Bulimia
    3. Irritable bowel syndrome
    4. Facetious hyperphagia

    Answer: B. Parotid gland enlargement and dental caries are common in bulimia, characterized by binge eating and purging.

45.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • The parotid duct crosses the masseter muscle and pierces the buccinator to open at the second upper molar.
  • The submandibular duct runs anteriorly between the mylohyoid and hyoglossus muscles, opening via caruncles lateral to the lingual frenulum.
  • The lingual nerve loops under the submandibular duct, and the hypoglossal nerve (CN XII) courses inferior to it.

References:

  • Marchal F, Dulguerov P. Sialolithiasis management: the state of the art. Arch Otolaryngol Head Neck Surg 2003;129:951–6.
  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:953–5, 1008–10.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 46, 53, 60–1.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:787–9.

Chapter 46: Berry Aneurysm

46.1 Approach to Vascular Supply of Brain

Learning Objectives:

  • Describe the course of the internal carotid and vertebral arteries.
  • List the major intracranial branches of the internal carotid and the basilar arteries.
  • Identify the components of the circle of Willis.

Definitions:

  • Angiography: Radiographic technique in which contrast medium is injected into the arterial system. X-ray images may be taken at regular intervals to follow the dye from the arterial supply through the venous drainage. Recent advances in MRI have made it possible to examine blood flow without injection of contrast medium.
  • Aneurysm: Disruption within the wall of an artery that fills with blood and inflates the muscular coat. The resulting dilation can exert pressure on surrounding structures and ultimately may rupture, leading to a rapid loss of blood pressure.
  • Syncope: Episode of fainting; a loss of consciousness not related to sleeping.

The arterial blood supply to the brain is derived from the paired internal carotid and paired vertebral arteries. The internal carotid arteries arise from the bifurcation of the common carotid arteries at about the level of the superior border of the thyroid cartilage. They are described as the direct continuation of the common carotids, have no branches in the neck, and ascend to the base of the skull, where they enter the carotid canal. The internal carotid arteries pass anteriorly and medially through the cavernous sinus to enter the cranial cavity and divide into their terminal branches, the anterior cerebral artery and the middle cerebral artery. The two anterior cerebral arteries join through the anterior communicating branch. The posterior communicating branch joins the middle cerebral with the posterior cerebral arteries.

The vertebral arteries are the first branches of the subclavian arteries in the root of the neck. They ascend through the transverse foramina of vertebrae C6 to C1, enter the cranial cavity through the foramen magnum, and unite to form the basilar artery near the junction of the pons and medulla. At the superior border of the pons, it divides into the posterior cerebral arteries. The chief intracranial branches of the vertebral arteries are the posterior inferior cerebellar arteries. Before its terminal bifurcation, the chief branches of the basilar artery are the anterior inferior cerebellar arteries, superior cerebellar arteries, and several pontine branches.

The cerebral arterial circle (of Willis) is the major anastomosis of the cerebral vasculature. This allows for perfusion of the brain even with arterial occlusion of one or more major arteries (such as carotid insufficiency). If the occlusion develops slowly, the anastomotic vessels will expand to compensate. However, the anastomosis may not be able to compensate if the occlusion develops rapidly, as with trauma. Blockage of one cerebral artery will have characteristic effects based on the region of the brain supplied by the vessel. The anterior cerebral artery supplies the medial surface of the cerebrum. The middle cerebral artery supplies the lateral surfaces, and the posterior cerebral artery supplies the inferior surface. The middle cerebral artery is occluded most often, presumably because it follows the same trajectory as the internal carotid.

46.2 Clinical Case: Berry Aneurysm

Case Study:

A 43-year-old man was washing his car when he suddenly complained of a severe headache and then slumped to the ground. His son, who had witnessed the episode, stated that his father grabbed his head with both hands and cried out in pain as he was falling. The son said that his father had no medical problems and exercised regularly. On examination in the emergency department, the patient was lethargic but responsive to deep pain stimuli. His pupils were dilated bilaterally and sluggishly reactive to light. CT scan of the head showed a significant intracranial hemorrhage. An angiogram demonstrated leakage of dye from the junction of the right internal carotid artery and the circle of Willis.

  • Most likely diagnosis: Ruptured berry aneurysm.
  • Clinical anatomy for this event: Weakness of the intracranial arterial junction.

Summary: An otherwise healthy 43-year-old man suddenly complained of a severe headache and lost consciousness. He is lethargic, responsive to deep pain, and has bilaterally dilated and sluggishly reactive pupils. CT imaging showed a significant intracranial hemorrhage, and an angiogram demonstrated leakage of dye from the junction of the right internal carotid artery and the circle of Willis.

Clinical Correlation: This otherwise healthy 43-year-old man had an acute and significant cerebral event. He had a severe headache quickly followed by loss of consciousness. There was no motor activity to suggest an epileptic seizure. Further, his comatose state ruled out self-limited etiologies such as syncope due to a vasovagal reaction. The cerebral imaging confirms intracranial hemorrhage. The possibilities include an arteriovenous malformation (a tangle of vessels that sometimes rupture) or a hemorrhagic stroke. The arteriogram shows leakage of dye from the junction of the internal carotid artery and the circle of Willis, strongly suggesting a berry aneurysm. The blood supply to the brain is derived from the paired internal carotid and the paired vertebral arteries. Occlusion of even one of these vessels would cause severe damage were it not for the anastomosis between these four vessels known as the circle of Willis. However, there is inherent weakness at the junction of the arteries, and an outpouching of the arterial wall, a berry aneurysm, may occur and ultimately rupture. The best treatment for such a ruptured aneurysm is surgical clip ligation. Medications such as calcium channel blockers are useful for preventing coexisting arterial vasospasm.

46.3 Comprehension Questions

  1. A 53-year-old man is being examined for carotid bruits. The physician would like to auscultate the internal carotid artery. At what level does the carotid artery usually bifurcate into the internal and external carotid arteries?
    1. At the level of the cricoid cartilage
    2. At the level of the thyroid cartilage
    3. At the level of sternal notch
    4. At the level of the C8 vertebra

    Answer: B. The carotid artery bifurcates at the level of the thyroid cartilage.

  2. A 64-year-old man is diagnosed with an acute stroke. His main deficit is a partial loss of his visual field. The neurologist diagnoses a lesion of the occipital lobe. Which of the following arteries is likely to be involved?
    1. Internal carotid
    2. External carotid
    3. Middle cerebral
    4. Posterior cerebral

    Answer: D. The occipital lobes are supplied by the posterior cerebral arteries, which are terminal branches of the basilar artery.

  3. A 35-year-old man complains of the worst headache of his life, grabs onto the back of his neck, and then slumps onto the floor. At the hospital, his CT findings are consistent with a subarachnoid hemorrhage. Which of the following is the most likely etiology?
    1. Carotid artery occlusion
    2. Vertebrobasilar artery occlusion
    3. Middle meningeal artery laceration
    4. Rupture of a berry aneurysm

    Answer: D. The most common causes of subarachnoid hemorrhage are rupture of a berry aneurysm in the circle of Willis and bleeding from an arteriovenous malformation.

46.4 Anatomy Pearls

  • The internal carotid arteries have no branches in the neck.
  • The terminal branches of the internal carotid arteries are the anterior and middle cerebral arteries.
  • The blood supply to the cerebellum is derived from the vertebrobasilar arterial system.
  • The circle of Willis allows anastomosis of the arterial blood supply of the brain.
  • The major arterial blood supply of the brain is from the internal carotid and vertebral arteries.

References:

  • Moore KL, Dalley AF. Clinically Oriented Anatomy, 5th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2006:927–32.
  • Netter FH. Atlas of Human Anatomy, 4th ed. Philadelphia, PA: Saunders/Elsevier, 2006: plates 139–143.
  • Snell RS. Clinical Anatomy by Regions, 8th ed. Baltimore, MD: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins, 2008:689–90, 750–1.

Chapter 47: Middle Ear Effusion

47.1 Approach to the Ear

Learning Objectives:

  • Describe the anatomy of the external acoustic meatus (canal).
  • Describe the anatomy of the tympanic membrane and the three ear ossicles.
  • Identify the structures of the middle ear cavity and those that communicate with it.

Definitions:

  • Insufflation: Act of blowing a powder or gas into a body cavity, in this case through the otoscope, to assess whether there is fluid in the middle ear.
  • Perilymph/endolymph: The bony labyrinth of the inner ear contains the membranous labyrinth. Within the lumen of the membranous ducts is endolymph, a fluid similar in composition to intracellular fluid (low sodium, high potassium). The space between the ducts and the bony walls is filled with perilymph, a fluid similar in composition to normal extracellular fluid (high sodium, low potassium). The compartments that are filled with perilymph and endolymph do not communicate.
  • Effusion: Spread of a liquid into a space. In this case, the fluid is from the inflammatory response to the allergy.
  • Myringotomy: Procedure in which the tympanic membrane is pierced and tubes are inserted into the opening to drain the exudate.

The external ear is composed of the auricle, an elastic cartilage structure covered with skin and having several named parts, one of which is the concha, which funnels sound waves down the external acoustic meatus or canal. The meatus is lined with skin, and the wall of the lateral third is cartilaginous, whereas the medial two-thirds are bony. It has an anteromedial S-shaped course, which can be straightened by posterosuperior traction on the auricle.

The medial end of the meatus is closed by the tympanic membrane, a somewhat cone-shaped, 1-cm membrane composed of collagen and elastic fibers that is covered externally by thin skin and lined internally by the mucous membrane of the middle ear. The apex of the membrane’s cone is called the umbo. The reflected light of an otoscope, the cone of light, originates at the umbo and is directed anteroinferiorly. A process of the malleus, called the handle, is applied to the medial surface of the membrane, and its tip is also attached at the umbo. The malleus has a lateral process that bulges the superior portion of the membrane laterally. The portion superior to the lateral process is the pars flaccida, and the remainder of the membrane is called the pars tensa.

The three ear ossicles are the malleus, incus, and stapes, lateral to medial, across the tympanic or middle ear cavity. Each bone has distinctive features. With a normal tympanic membrane, the handle of the malleus is clearly visible, and the long process of the incus is often visible posterior to the malleus. The stapes is shaped much like a stirrup, and its footplate fits into the oval window on the medial wall of the tympanic cavity. Its in-and-out movement transmits pressure waves through the endolymph within the cochlea, where the nerve impulses for hearing are generated. Excessive movements of the ear ossicles with loud noise are dampened by the tensor tympani muscle attached to the malleus and the stapedius muscle attached to the stapes. These muscles are innervated by CN V and CN VII, respectively.

The tympanic cavity is contained within the petrous portion of the temporal bone. Its features are usually described as being contained within a box with a roof, four walls, and a floor. The tympanic cavity is lined with a mucous membrane and contains the chorda tympani branch of CN VII and the tympanic plexus (CN IX) in addition to the ear ossicles and their associated muscles. Air pressure within the cavity is equalized with the nasopharynx through the pharyngotympanic or auditory tube.

Table 47-1: Walls of the Tympanic Cavity
RoofFloorLateral WallMedial WallAnterior WallPosterior Wall
Bony feature of temporal boneTegmen tympaniTympanic membrane, prominence of malleus, epitympanic recessPromontory, prominence of facial canal, prominence of lateral semicircular canalMastoid process, pyramid
Related structureMiddle cranial fossaInternal jugular veinExternal acoustic meatus, chorda tympaniVestibular apparatus, CN VIICarotid artery, tensor tympani muscleMastoid air cells, CN VII, stapedius muscle
